  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2345940652" />
    <a title="Michael Ferro" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6105365-michael-ferro"><img alt="Michael Ferro" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1509421150p2/6105365.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2345940652?book_show_action=true">Apr 11, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael Ferro" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael Ferro" href="/user/show/6105365-michael-ferro">Michael Ferro</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2345940652" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8649929515141407334">Jenny Offill's DEPT. OF SPECULATION is a smorgasbord of delightfully mindful appetizers that all come together as one infinitely satisfying meal. At times hilarious, other times sad, though sometimes both, this slim novel defies much of the trope stylings of a modern doomed marriage story, instead providing the reader with an endless supply of little food for thought nuggets of wisdom and whimsey mixed among the narrative of a marriage crumbling before our eyes. <br/><br/>Offill has weaved plenty of specu</span>
  <span id="freeText8649929515141407334" style="display:none">Jenny Offill's DEPT. OF SPECULATION is a smorgasbord of delightfully mindful appetizers that all come together as one infinitely satisfying meal. At times hilarious, other times sad, though sometimes both, this slim novel defies much of the trope stylings of a modern doomed marriage story, instead providing the reader with an endless supply of little food for thought nuggets of wisdom and whimsey mixed among the narrative of a marriage crumbling before our eyes. <br /><br />Offill has weaved plenty of speculation into the plot here, which offers rich passages of "deeper reading" for those who don't mind constantly pausing while reading in order to ponder an uncanny musing or strange turn of phrase (and I for one don't mind it at all). In the end, DEPT. OF SPECULATION is a book about life and wonder, about bitter disappointment and the realities of life that often get lost in the minutia, and those who read it will likely find themselves highlighting plenty of sections to come back to and think upon during a dreary, rainy day.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8649929515141407334" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText6359090612749457551" style="display:none"><br />Written in a blog diary style, these are bittersweet dispatches from life on the frontline of a failing marriage. All the agonies and joys of motherhood are here too, condensed into unforgettable moments that any woman who has ever been ‘on the edge’ will empathise with, even if you have never had to walk a baby to sleep for hours.<br /><br />Extract<br />And that phrase – 'sleeping like a baby'. Some blonde said it blithely on the subway the other day. I wanted to lie down next to her and scream for five hours in her ear...<br />I ordered a CD online that promised to put even the most colicky baby to sleep. I sounded like a giant heart beating. As if you had been forced to live inside such a heart with no possibility of escape.<br />Our friend R stopped by one night to see us while it was playing. 'Wow. That is some bad techno music,' he said. He sat on the couch and drank beer while I paced with the baby...<br />Thump, thump, thump, thump went the music. But the heartbeat song only enraged the baby. On and on she screamed. 'This is intense,' R said after an hour or two. R who is not our friend anymore and began not to be on the night in question.<br /><br />Reviewed for Whichbook.net<br /></span>

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7828774595121733074"><i>Dept. Of Speculation</i> is a modern, contemporary novel told from the perspective of a thirty something-year-old American woman, a writer, who lives in New York with her sound engineer husband and young daughter. A seemingly perfect husband and a good marriage face a huge hurdle when the husband falls in love/lust with a much younger woman - the ultimate cliche. <br/><br/>The vignettes and thought fragments made for a very interesting read. Despite its brevity, it's a compelling analysis of long term relatio</span>
  <span id="freeText7828774595121733074" style="display:none"><i>Dept. Of Speculation</i> is a modern, contemporary novel told from the perspective of a thirty something-year-old American woman, a writer, who lives in New York with her sound engineer husband and young daughter. A seemingly perfect husband and a good marriage face a huge hurdle when the husband falls in love/lust with a much younger woman - the ultimate cliche. <br /><br />The vignettes and thought fragments made for a very interesting read. Despite its brevity, it's a compelling analysis of long term relationships. The writing was very accessible but that only made it more poignant.<br /><br />My library promised to purchase Offill's latest novel, so I'm looking forward to reading it.</span>

  <span id="freeText1089867550287160335" style="display:none"><i>Advise for wives circa 1896: The indiscriminate reading of novels is one of the most injurious habits to which a married woman can be subject. Besides the false view of human nature it will impart … it produces an indifference to the performance of domestic duties, and contempt for ordinary realities.</i><br /><br />As discussed on the Radio 4 Book Club discussion of Jenny Offill's second novel <br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.bbc.co.uk/sounds/play/m000h0vj">https://www.bbc.co.uk/sounds/play/m00...</a><br /><br />A discussion which includes a guest appearance by me asking about the space theme.<br /><br />After also joining the Radio 4 Front Row Booker Book Group – perhaps I am becoming a radio monster? <br /><br /><i>“Road not taken”</i> my children say<br /><br /><i>“I think I must have missed your second book” he says<br />“No” I say “There isn’t one” <br />He looks uncomfortable; both of us are calculating the years, or maybe only I am</i><br /><br />The Second edition of the Folio Prize, in 2015, was won by Akhil Sharma with “Family Live”. The author’s second novel it is an autobiographical novel set in New York. It was written 14 years after his first novel. Also longlisted was Jenny Offill’s Dept. of Speculation. The author’s second novel is an auto-fictional novel set in New York. It was written 14 years after her second novel.<br /><br />Another longlisted book as Ben Lerner’s 10:04, an autofictional novel set in New York and Rachel Cusk’s “Outline”, part of her autofictional trilogy. Unfortunately, the third in that series so that the pattern is broken. <br /><br />The husband makes soundscapes of New York City. <i>“What does that even mean” the wife asks.</i> <br /><br />“Just like in Lost Children Archive” the reviewer observes. <br /><br />Can the book convey the emotions of new motherhood.<br /><br /><i>The baby’s eyes were dark, almost black, and when I nursed her in the middle of the night, she’s stare at me with a stunned, shipwrecked look as if my body was the island she’d washed up on.<br /><br />The days with baby felt long but there was nothing expansive about them. Caring for her required me to repeat a series of tasks that has the peculiar quality of seeming both urgent and tedious. They cut up the day into little scraps<br /><br />But the smell of her hair? The way she clasped her hand around my fingers. This was like medicine. For once I didn’t have to think. The animal was ascendant.</i><br /><br />And says the reviewer, when does their relationship, six years later, deteriorate.<br /><br /><i>It is important if someone asks you to remember one of your happiest ties to consider not only the question but the questioner. You say that this fits perhaps fits a book that is about exploration of inner space.<br /><br />The reviewer says “I think a better link is how spacemen confined together in close quarters for extended periods, like trapped polar explorers, are in fact an analogy for the pressures of a marriage, especially when a young child constricts your practical universe.”<br /><br /><i>I went only as far as Rite Aid. Rite Aid was only a block from our apartment. It was exactly the distance I could make in the freezing cold, carrying the baby in my arms. Also the farthest distance I could sprint if she started screaming again and I had to go home.</i><br /><br />I think, the concept is very different form rescue distance as discussed in Samantha Schweblin’s “Fever Dream”. <br /><br />Also discussed in “Lost Children Archive” the reviewer observes.<br /><br />“Unique” I say. "Both elliptical and aphoristic" <br /><br />Advise for Goodreaders circa 2020: The indiscriminate reading of this novel is one of the most beneficial habits to which a person can be subject. Besides the deep view of 21st Century human nature it will impart … it produces a heightened awareness of ordinary realities. <br /><br />Domestic duties may unfortunately suffer.<br /><br /><i>“What are you reading about”? the husband asks her from across the room. “Weather” she tells him.</i><br /><br />"Jenny Offill’s “Weather”" – the reviewer says, probably tomorrow.</span>

<div id="review_1880135343" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1880135343" />
    <a title="Mariah Roze" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22371819-mariah-roze"><img alt="Mariah Roze" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1496763524p2/22371819.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1880135343?book_show_action=true">Jan 22, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mariah Roze" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mariah Roze" href="/user/show/22371819-mariah-roze">Mariah Roze</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1880135343" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17765912284167301051">Well this book broke me into pieces. I just felt super connected to the main character and even though I am not married and I don't have any children I was still able to put myself in that characters shoes. The author did a great job at this. <br/><br/>This was a fast, short, easy read that was super interesting and made you think and feel greatly!</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div class='carouselRow' style='width: 3600px'>
<ul>
<li class='cover' id='bookCover_51541496'>
<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/51541496-luster"><img alt="Luster" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/books/1568668703l/51541496._SX98_SY160_.jpg" />

<script type="text/javascript" charset="utf-8">
//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('dnbsQQq///SPmBVzA8G/ndPsK5TbYQnxAP28oq9UIGZKvPCCWsR8JYsvtN8JNUWGWMHkrEtz8S1k3tWjEF18UA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('h1lrhnJfQZKmmzqWSF06jmtN6YlBaj6vcJBeFLUAMsu7k3dFIiTCQ6IsmzpCqcCV4GAmsdF4xnMUszcVCglu/Q==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

Nobody will ever know who killed her. It wasn\'t me. Here is her dead body&quot;. Our narrator is deeply shaken; she has no idea what to make of this. She is new to area, having moved her from her longtime home after the death of her husband, and she knows very few people. And she\'s a little shaky even on best days. Her brooding about this note quickly grows into a full-blown obsession, and she begins to devote herself to exploring the possibilities of her conjectures about who this woman was and how she met her fate. Her suppositions begin to find echoes in the real world, and with mounting excitement and dread, the fog of mystery starts to form into a concrete and menacing shape. But as we follow her in her investigation, strange dissonances start to accrue, and our faith in her grip on reality weakens, until finally, just as she seems be facing some of the darkness in her own past with her late husband, we are forced to face the prospect that there is either a more innocent explanation for all this or a much more sinister one - one that strikes closer to home.\n\nA triumphant blend of horror, suspense, and pitch-black comedy, \'Death in Her Hands\' asks us to consider how the stories we tell ourselves both guide us closer to the truth and keep us at bay from it. An introverted young man from Alabama, black and queer, he has left behind his family without escaping the long shadows of his childhood. For reasons of self-preservation, Wallace has enforced a wary distance even within his own circle of friends—some dating each other, some dating women, some feigning straightness. But over the course of a late-summer weekend, a series of confrontations with colleagues, and an unexpected encounter with an ostensibly straight, white classmate, conspire to fracture his defenses But after growing up together in a small, southern black community and running away at age sixteen, it\'s not just the shape of their daily lives that is different as adults, it\'s everything: their families, their communities, their racial identities. Many years later, one sister lives with her black daughter in the same southern town she once tried to escape. The other secretly passes for white, and her white husband knows nothing of her past. Still, even separated by so many miles and just as many lies, the fates of the twins remain intertwined. What will happen to the next generation, when their own daughters\' storylines intersect?\n\nWeaving together multiple strands and generations of this family, from the Deep South to California, from the 1950s to the 1990s, Brit Bennett produces a story that is at once a riveting, emotional family story and a brilliant exploration of the American history of passing. Looking well beyond issues of race, The Vanishing Half considers the lasting influence of the past as it shapes a person\'s decisions, desires, and expectations, and explores some of the multiple reasons and realms in which people sometimes feel pulled to live as something other than their origins.\n\nAs with her New York Times-bestselling debut The Mothers, Brit Bennett offers an engrossing Ignoring her feminist leanings and her better instincts, Ava finds herself moving into Julian’s apartment, letting him buy her clothes, and, eventually, striking up a sexual relationship with him. When Julian’s job takes him back to London, she stays put, unsure where their relationship stands.\n\nEnter Edith. Ava has been carefully pretending that Julian is nothing more than an absentee roommate, so when Julian announces that he’s returning to Hong Kong, she faces a fork in the road. Should she return to the easy compatibility of her life with Julian or take a leap into the unknown with Edith?\n\nPolitically alert, heartbreakingly raw, and dryly funny, Exciting Times is thrillingly attuned to the great freedoms and greater uncertainties of modern love. She escapes her lot by marrying a rich man, but having gained a husband, a house, high society, and a maid, she finds that her new life of privilege is no less constrained. Not only has she taken up different forms of time-consuming labor—social and erotic—but she is now, however passively, forcing other women to clean up after her. Perhaps another and more drastic solution is necessary?\n\nReminiscent of a lost Victorian classic in miniature, yet taking equal inspiration from such modern authors as Jean Rhys, Octavia Butler, Clarice Lispector, and Jean Genet, Amina Cain\'s Indelicacy is at once a ghost story without a ghost, a fable without a moral, and a down-to-earth investigation of the barriers faced by women in both life and literature. Arch, yet warm; aspiring and impervious; confiding and enigmatic; reposing and intrepid; Cain has conjured a protagonist who purged my mind and filled my heart.&quot; —Claire-Louise Bennett, author of <span id="freeTextContainer17715424917315020034">Jenny Offill is an American author born in Massachusetts. Her first novel Last Things was published in 1999 was a New York Times Notable book and a finalist for the L.A Times First Book Award.<br/><br/>She is also the co-editor with Elissa Schappell of two anthologies of essays and the author of several children's books She teaches in the MFA programs at Brooklyn College, Columbia University and Queens Univ</span>
  <span id="freeText17715424917315020034" style="display:none">Jenny Offill is an American author born in Massachusetts. Her first novel Last Things was published in 1999 was a New York Times Notable book and a finalist for the L.A Times First Book Award.<br /><br />She is also the co-editor with Elissa Schappell of two anthologies of essays and the author of several children's books She teaches in the MFA programs at Brooklyn College, Columbia University and Queens University.</span>
